Date:  Sat, 01 Jan 2005 17:24:21 -0000
From:  Dennis Persyk 
Subject:  Q2 Machholz Movie

I imaged Q2 Machholz and have a movie showing its motion across the
sky during a 96 minute period. The comet was clearly visible under
the full moon with the C-11 scope at 93x, but quite difficult to
find in the 7x50 binoculars. Transparency was 2/10 Pickering with
high thin clouds.

Finder chart, isophotes, movie, image and supporting data at
http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

Thank you for visiting my site.

Clear skies,

Dennis Persyk
Igloo Observatory Home Page http://dpersyk.home.att.net
Hampshire, IL

Date:  Sun, 02 Jan 2005 19:22:26 -0500
From:  KCStarguy@aol.com
Subject:  Comet Machholz in the Evening Sky
FYi if you have not seen this spectacular pic

Comet Machholz in the Evening Sky
By Greg Bryant and Alan M. MacRobert
http://skyandtelescope.com/observing/objects/comets/article_1396_1.asp

One of the nicest photos I have seen of the comet shows the following

"In photographs, Comet Machholz (C/2004 Q2) has been showing a blue gas tail
and the edge of an orange-yellowish dust tail emerging at very different
angles from the coma. Johannes Schedler in Austria took this picture on December
12, 2004, using a Canon EF 200mm f/2.8 lens on an SBIG STL-11000M CCD
camera. Courtesy Johannes Schedler."

If you have comet photos, illustrations, ccd pics or combination, you
possibly can win the Astronomical League silver award certificate and comet pin.
Contact the websites below or contact me for further information.

Happy New Year

Dr. Eric Flescher (kcstarguy@aol.com),

Olathe, KS. USA,

Dr. Eric Flescher (kcstarguy@aol.com),

Hi Terry,
You've captured a nice long tail. I'm constantly amazed by how sensitive
the 300D is!  Well processed too.

Coincidentally, I imaged the comet at exactly the same time last night,
but at a longer focal length (200mm).
See http://www.tip.net.au/~vello/2004q2/20041228.jpg for the inner coma
detail. The gaps in the star trials are from excluding images that
contained satellite trails (15 of the 29 original 60 sec images were
discarded!)

Cheers,
Vello

Date:  Sun, 02 Jan 2005 20:52:48 -0800
From:  Charles Bell 
Subject:  New Year's Comet
C/2004 Q2-Machholz 01012005 0408UT

http://www.quantumhyperspace.com/imagefiles/C2004Q2-01012005-0408UT.JPG

60 x 30 sec 1x1 binning
ST-2000 + MiniBorg60

This was on New Year's eve local time amid all the
neighboring hoopla and their fireworks. It sounded
like I was under seige from all sides.

This has been my best resolution on the dust tail.
Seems like shorter exposures work better since the
comet is moving so fast.

Charles Bell
Vicksburg, Mississippi USA

Date:  Wed, 05 Jan 2005 17:49:31 +0100
From:  "gvnn64\@libero\.it" 
Subject:  Re:[Comet-Images] New file uploaded to Comet-Images
Hello,
this is an image of C/2004 Q2 (Machholz) I obtained a couple of nights ago with some narrowband filters. Each original frame was treated with an azimuthally-normalized alghorythm, in order to enhance some details within the coma. It seems that in the CN there was a couple of jets, not visible in the C2 or dust continuum.
Cheers,
Giovanni Sostero (Remanzacco Observatory, Italy)

Rick. I am sure it would. It is a fine line to walk with an image
like this one which only has 60 seconds each of RGBL. Add in the
fact that the chip in this camera is known for noise and the line
between detail maintained and a smooth image really comes down to
personal taste. I do agree that a smoother look is nice and
therefore I have yet another version which I am posting to see if you
think it feels better. Thanks for your input because that is what
this board was created for. I have to say that with the great images
from Michael and Gerald we all have work to do to try and keep pace.
See the link. Mike H.

Date:  Thu, 06 Jan 2005 17:12:09 -0000
From:  Rick Wiggins 
Subject:  Re: Comet Q2-2nd Color

Mike,
I fully understand the difficulity of getting the best image of this
comet and agree with your comments. My only attempt is at the link
below. Click on 2nd image down.
http://home.earthlink.net/~rickwiggins/index.html
I used a STL11k camera on FSQ with single LRGB 210sec exposures,
binned 3x3. The STL11k is a low QE camera. I was fighting between
showing detail in the tail and noise. The comet is now moving faster,
so I would not be able to capture in color using this method with
this camera. I was hoping for another attemp this month, but weather
has stopped all dry efforts here. Here is a link to a cool method of
comet capture and processing:
http://www.ewellobservatory.com/ccd/comet.cfm
It probably doesn't match your exact equipment, but contains many
good ideas.
I also did a side by side comparison of your 2 images on my LCD
monitor in photoshop. Visually (and on my monitor), I liked the
smoothed version a little better. It looks as if you were able to
hold all the detail, but still remove some noise.
Excellent work!
Rick

Michael and Gerald,
Another beauty. Great image and what a keeper! I hope you guys will

methodology. See Richard Bennion's monograph of his procedures:
http://www.ewellobservatory.com/ccd/comet.cfm
I did get to look at it with the binoculars last night as clouds
drifted by. Our visibility is now down to around 500 feet (I can
barely see the freeway below my office on the 10th floor). I had
planned to visit dark skies to have a go at the comet, but the dark
skies are only accessable via snowmobile and clouds expected for at
least another week. So keep the images coming, that we may follow
your progress.
Rick

Date:  Tue, 11 Jan 2005 08:43:53 +0100
From:  Maik Meyer 
Subject:  C/2003 T4 and M57
Hi list,

while planning morning observing sessions for C/2003 T4 (LINEAR) for the weekend
(I really hope I manage myself out of the bed...) I noticed that the comet will be
in the same field with M57, the ring nebula in Lyra. It is not listed in Seiichi's
comet rendezvous list because the distance is not that close. For those with wide
angle imaging capabilities it might be a nice shot to have the 11mag comet and the
nebula in the same field.

It will be at its closest to M57 on Jan. 16 with about 1 deg. By the way, on
February 12 it will pass M27 at about 3 deg and on February 15 it will pass M71
with about 1 deg.

I really hope my bed releases me (besides the co-operation of the weather). ;)

Cheers, Maik

Date:  Tue, 11 Jan 2005 18:42:40 +0100
From:  waldemar skorupa 
Subject:  q2 trip to france
Hello,

my friend Norbert and me traveled to southern France to take some images of
Q2 under good sky conditions.
For me every kilometer of 2500 done was worth to see Q2 under this sky!!!
We observed the comet on 4 nights(6-9th January).
On 8th and 9th January tail activity was not so spectacular like on 7th
January

Now we have a lot of imageprocessing to do.
Starting with a single shot 4mins using an EOS 20D and a 200mm telephoto:

http://www.schweifstern.de/images-pages/q2/07012005-q2-200er-ws.htm

Greetings
Waldemar

Date:  Wed, 12 Jan 2005 09:33:18 +1300
From:  John_Drummond 
Subject:  Re: [Comet-Images] Re: Webpage of Stardate 2005
Hi Mike,

yes 'Coops' (Ian Cooper) is a living legend here in New Zealand (besides
variable star/comet discoverer/1987A visual discoverer Albert Jones). Ian
knows so much about everything astronomical (and can relate to all ages in a
non arrogant way). One astronomer wrote this about Coops at Stardate...

Once again I marvel at Ian's expertise. i.e. I have recently installed a set
of Rudy Zondag's Palm DSC ( recommended ) on my 16". At one point on
Saturday night the cable was accidentally unplugged. I didn't need to plug
it back in and do a realignment until Ian moved off to another 'scope.

And so he should know the sky, he's written much (if not all) of the 'Night
Sky Observing Guide' for the Southern Hemisphere (volume 3) - soon to be
released.

If any society was rich enough, Ian would be a great speaker to sponsor to
come and speak.

Sorry if this is off-topic...

Cheers

John Drummond
Gisborne, New Zealand
E 178* 03', S 38* 40'
email: john_drummond@xtra.co.nz
website: http://www.possumobservatory.co.nz

Date:  Sat, 15 Jan 2005 05:08:19 -0000
From:  Dennis Persyk 
Subject:  Q2 Machholz Infopage

I imaged Q2 Machholz and have a movie showing its motion across the
sky during an 80 minute period. I have shrunk the movie to a mere
208 kB for my modem viewers, so please take a look.

Simulated eyepiece view through the NP101, finder chart, isophotes,
movie, image and additional supporting data at
http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

In my "simulated eyepiece view", a/k/a "digital sketch", I show a
faint greenish cast to the comet head. I viewed the comet with the
NP101 with eyepieces varying in focal length from 6.4 mm to 30 mm.
The 9 mm Nagler gave the best view for me under the moonlit sky.

It sure seemed to me that I saw a faint green color. Was I just
imagining it, or have other visual observers seen some color lately?

I have included a Larson-Sekanina filter view of the comet head.
This is a very powerful filter to bring out jets. I'd appreciate
feedback as to whether I've applied it properly. It certainly has
the potential to bring out artifacts if improperly applied.

Thank you for visiting my site.

Clear skies,

Dennis Persyk
Igloo Observatory Home Page http://dpersyk.home.att.net
Hampshire, IL

Date:  Tue, 18 Jan 2005 23:35:31 +0100
From:  Josef Mueller 
Subject:  9P/Tempel
Hi all,
... and @ Giovanni

my first email, my first picture to this group.
"Look out Comet Tempel1, here comes Deep Impact!"
A very faint comet (about 16 mag), moving 0.72"/min PA: 94 deg.
A short faint tail PA 281 deg.
Distance: 1.6 AU (245 960 213 km)
Blinking in Astrometrica I could find the comet on my singel shots of 30s
12" Newton, f/5, SXV H9 30 x 30s

My picture:
http://www.westerwald-astro.de/jjm/kometen/9p_tempel_inv_1.php

Cheers, Josef Mueller

PS: First link on my new website (in construction)

Westerwald-Sternwarte Irmtraut, #A21
Irmtraut, Germany

From:  ligustri rolando 
Subject:  Application of images of comet C2004Q2
Hi to everybody,

On last January 17 I have photographed the comet with mine apo 114/600,

ccd SXVH9 in bin 2x2, every minute from 19:36TU to 20:11 TU.

At 19:39 o'clock I have begun to take back a strange phenomenon that you can see to this
address: http://comete.uai.it/comete/variazioni.jpg

I premise that the ccd was power on from over a time and that the objective of the
telescope had not misted. What can that be from me photographed?

Is there someone who has some images done around the time of my photo?

Thanks.

Rolando Ligustri

CAST: Circolo AStrofili Talmassons
www.castfvg.it/fotoccd.htm#comete

Date:  Wed, 19 Jan 2005 19:51:04 -0000
From:  Dennis Persyk 
Subject:  Re: Application of images of comet C2004Q2

Hi Rolando,

I have *exactly* the same effect visible in my movie! See
http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

My mentor explained that it is due to frost forming on the MX716
cover glass. Unfortunately, it is quite likely do to water vapor
inside the chip cavity. The quick fix (for my MX716) is a blast
from a hair dryer; the long-term fix is to put the camera in a
bottle with a fresh desiccant and let it sit for several days. The
water will eventually come out by the process of diffusion.

Clear skies,

Dennis Persyk
Igloo Observatory Home Page http://dpersyk.home.att.net
Hampshire, IL

New Images http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

Date:  Wed, 19 Jan 2005 23:21:19 -0000
From:  tricks46 
Subject:  Re: Application of images of comet C2004Q2

I would point out only my experience with the SBIG camera!!. The
opening of the hole does not start at the first of the images, but
during the middle. It starts and then concludes after a few frames.
A dust mote would be there from the start to a certain extent, so I
would not rule this out but look elsewhere if a flat was applied to
each frame seperately. Frost will start when camera starts to cools
down. Could it be frost? I don't think so. Frost will start and end
as a distortion. Frost appears as a dark area or a clear area with a
starting point which seems to grow as the frost grows on the chip or
covering . Frost distorts, and yet this image or images, of the
images actually seems to clear up the affected area through the dust
tail. If you look at image taken 14:53 the edge becomes flat on the
right side. and then receeds after in the next frames. So what would
cause the area of the dust tail to clear up and leave the stars
intact? Could it be the comet clearing the path in the dust tail?
The flat area of the above image makes me think no but not a lot of
this seems to apply to the regular ideas of imaging. Good luck
Rolando, this is yet another learning tool for the members. Mike H

Date:  Wed, 19 Jan 2005 23:57:06 -0000
From:  tricks46 
Subject:  Re: Application of images of comet C2004Q2

After thinking about this for a bit, my brain has come up with a
small possible solution. All of the images are uniform in the start
and after the area appears. All flat are are applied and nothing has
been done differently to cause this area.
The flat area in image 14:53 has kept me going back to look. If a
brief but undectected area of light appeared from one side, would
this not cause such a centered area to be detected in only a few of
the images? Growing from one side as the light grew in strength and
then receding as it passed? This would also account for the flat
side to the area. It could be almost undecteable, except, by the ccd
chip and would show up as a flaw in the finished image. Images
except for the ones that had the extra light input would look ok.
Other than the comet clearing an area itself in the dust tail, this
is all I can say which might be one solution. Mike H.

Date:  Fri, 21 Jan 2005 05:21:38 +1300
From:  John_Drummond 
Subject:  OBS:V-C/2005 A1 (LINEAR)
Hi all,

just observed and imaged newly discovered comet C/2005 A1 (LINEAR)...

C/2005 A1 (LINEAR) 2005 January 20.62 UT: m1 = 13.4, Dia.= 1'.0, DC = 4, 41cm f4.5 Reflect (107x)...John Drummond, (Gisborne, New Zealand) [New comet. Small, indistinct coma. Slight central condensation. No tail visually - but clearly seen photographically (about 2' in PA of 295*). Rapid movement. 48* alt in the E. Stellar LM =15.6 (scope)].

Comet details and image -

http://www.possumobservatory.co.nz/comets-c-2005a1(linear).htm

or

http://tinyurl.com/4okva

Cheers.

John Drummond
Gisborne, New Zealand
E 178* 03', S 38* 40'
email: john_drummond@xtra.co.nz
website: http://www.possumobservatory.co.nz

Date:  Sun, 23 Jan 2005 23:56:04 +0200
From:  Mike Begbie 
Subject:  Re: [Comet-Images] new member
Hi Alex

Hmmmm... excellent images with this veteran camera! I too have a MTL3 in pristine condition, I collect old cameras. Did you know that this camera is apparently quite valuable as a collector's item? I also have two original Nikon F's (not a scratch on them) with about nine Nikkormat lenses...these are apparently worth a fortune and this camera has it's own dedicated website! The shots that I get from these, as I am also a professional terrestrial photographer are stunning...no digital camera that has less than 6 megapixels can compare, I have found. As far as I know, (and the group can correct me if I am wrong)Nikon F's were used on the Gemini missions and, together with Leica and Hasselblad, also on the Apollo missions. These cameras of mine have seen a lot of comets, from Kohoutek in '73 up until Machholz in 2005! It's great to be able to image with cameras that require no batteries, and so much less expensive!!! Here, two batteries for my Canon EOS 2000 Rebel cost Z$600,000.00!!! That's a weeks' groceries for two people in this country. Welcome to the group.

Regards

Mike Begbie
Harare
Zimbabwe
Dark Sky Africa!

Date:  Tue, 25 Jan 2005 23:31:59 +1030
From:  Michael Mattiazzo 
Subject:  Comet Machholz jet activity Jan 7th 2005
Hello all,

Finally got around to processing some images of comet Machholz.
Jan 7th at 12:20UT
40x10 second exposures, Starlight Express MX7c imager + C11 at f/3.3
Larsen-Sekania filter applied. Note the 2 prominent jets (6 and 7 o'clock
positions) in the sunward direction. The ion tail streamer points towards
the upper right.

http://www.yp-connect.net/~mmatti/

Cheers,
Michael Mattiazzo 

Date:  Tue, 25 Jan 2005 13:26:20 -0000
From:  gvnn64 
Subject:  Re: Comet Machholz jet activity Jan 7th 2005

> Finally got around to processing some images of comet Machholz.
> Jan 7th at 12:20UT
> 40x10 second exposures, Starlight Express MX7c imager + C11 at f/3.3
> Larsen-Sekania filter applied. Note the 2 prominent jets (6 and 7
o'clock
> positions) in the sunward direction. The ion tail streamer points
towards
> the upper right.

Hello Michael,
yes, comet Machholz inner coma seems to be pretty active! I have
imaged this structures on several nights using narrowband filters,
and it seems that the feature is mainly produced by dust, possibly
dragged off by gas emissions emanating from the active areas on the
nucleus. I will post later the link to a recent image, where this
stuff is evidenced by means of several image processing procedures.
Cheers,
Giovanni Sostero (Remanzacco Obs, CARA)

the noise untill Guide showed the comet was on top of two faint nebula
(on a scale of 1 to 6, these are both rated 5). LBN 679 and VDB 10.
I know this isn't all the noise but, they are causing problems.
Thanks, Mike H.

I recently bought 2 Canon Digital Rebels (6M pixel), with 24mm/1.4L,
50mm/1.4, 200mm/2.8L, 15mm/2.8, 100mm/2.8 (macro), 1.4x TC. (along with
Manual Focus 50mm/1.2L, 85mm/1.2L). Also, have adapters to use Nikon
300mm/2.8, 180mm/2.8 (& my other Nikon lenses down to 16mm/2.8 fisheye)
with an ST2000 (same camera Mike has) using Mandel WFA. I have an
Astrophysics Traveler (4.1" f6..610mm focal-length, plus .75 TC). This
along with my film cameras: Pentax 6x7, 8" f1.5 Schmidt Camera (TP2415 &
E100G).

Unfortunately, I'm extremely distracted with work...missed a number of
clear days. It has been clear (but very windy) since Sat. So, I missed
the above! (very disapppointing, since my reputation is getting comets
on clear days here in Mojave desert..see
http://www.comet-track.com/hb/hb.html ). I think I can get out tonight
for a "quickie"..just film with 8" Schmidt Camera (TP2415 & color). Can
do the one-shot color with Canon D300 & lens assortment. I wish Mike
could handle my ST2000..still has never took an astrophoto!! (had this
camera for 2 years now).

Hey Mike, if I send you my equipment (ST2000 with CFW, Mandel WFA, Nikon
300mm/2.8, 180mm/2.8, 105mm/2.5, 50mm/1.4, Robofocus)..can you handle
the workload? I have my hands full with work, not to mention driving
out 120+ miles (3hr drive minimum..ONE WAY) do run 2 systems: film (8"
Schmidt camera) PLUS Canon D300 (2 bodies with lens assortment).
Probably, not physically possible, since that's at least 6 hrs of
driving. Wait, I can actually goto another site..only 90 min drive
one-way. That's more feasible. STILL, I'm burdened with work

[ see http://www.jumplive.com , click on "real time webcast
Laughlin"..when I was out in Arizona on Jan 18/19 for this OffRoadRace,
I got one night of shooting..wide field of comet ]

Ever since then, it's been moonlight or rain..but it cleared up this
last Saturday (VERY windy & cold). Missed Sunday, which is maddening.
Probably will go out tonight, despite some work committments.

I REALLY need someone to use my ST2000+CFW (Nikon 300mm/2.8 &
Astrophysics 610mm f6 + .75 TC) in southern California..or someone like
Mike elsewhere, who has a *strong* committment like myself. Any ideas?
Yeah, I could ship the equipment out to a deserving comet
astrophotographer. I think I just answered my question while
typing..Martin Germano. He's been chomping to get into CCD, so here's a

Date:  Thu, 03 Feb 2005 15:51:40 -0500
From:  stevepacs@aol.com
Subject:  Re: [Comet-Images] Re: New Member, Steve Cox at Rocky Top Observatory RTO
Hello Charles,
That was an exciting night, and I was experimenting at the time I took
the images.
I set up my 35 MM Minolta XE 7 with 400 speed color film, on a tripod with a
wide angle 28 mm lens, and an extended shutter button. After pointing I
would manually hold the shutter open for from 30 seconds to 2 minutes per
exposure. After I had my pictures developed I scanned the color negatives at 1200
dots/inch. My scanner is a Cannon 5000 F, and you can scan color negatives, and
print out color prints. I then loaded the bitmap files into image composer
and enlarged, and cropped out the better meteors I had captured. Some
negatives had up to 4 meteors on them. It was an experiment, but it worked great, the
color is real too. There were times during that storm you could count 3
mentors at the same instant, that helped a lot.The pictures on the web site are
from 40 exposures, This was a lot of fun, I wished I had had 4 cameras going
at the same time, I missed some great ones.
Hope this helps.
Best Regards, Steve Cox

Date:  Fri, 11 Feb 2005 02:53:03 -0000
From:  tricks46 
Subject:  Q4-Mosaic in Grayscale

Well, finally got to a point that I was satisified with. It is a
composit of 36x60 second images either full frame or partial frame.
Took 24 hours of on/off work to remove gradients and try to smooth
out the noise. Ended up with one piece of space trash in one frame
but as they say, it gives character, I guess. It is reduced 50% from
the original and still is 1200 pix wide. I will sure miss this comet
unless someone can find me another one to start on. How about it
Don? Thanks, Mike H.

Date:  Mon, 14 Feb 2005 18:06:05 +1300
From:  John_Drummond 
Subject:  OBS:V - C/2005 A1 (LINEAR) - and an image of it
Hi all,

observed C/2005 A1 (LINEAR) two nights ago...

C/2005 A1 (LINEAR) 2005 February 12.50 UT: m1 = 10.3, Dia.= 2'.0, DC = 4, 41cm f4.5 Reflect (83x). John Drummond, (Gisborne, New Zealand) [A1 appears as a small smudge of haze. It's somewhat brighter than a few weeks ago. Has a small coma with some slight central condensation - has a larger haze around it? No tail visually - but clearly seen photographically. Rapid movement. 33* alt in the SE. Stellar LM =15.5 (scope)].

I also imaged it...

http://www.possumobservatory.co.nz/c-2005a1-linear-10x1min@800asa-41cm@f4,5-050212-text2.jpg

or

http://tinyurl.com/6xjvl

Other images and details re this comet can be seen at...

http://www.possumobservatory.co.nz/comets-c-2005a1(linear).htm

Cheers.

John Drummond
Gisborne, New Zealand
E 178* 03', S 38* 40'
email: john_drummond@xtra.co.nz
website: http://www.possumobservatory.co.nz

Date:  Mon, 14 Feb 2005 16:21:50 -0000
From:  adastragrl 
Subject:  Deep Impact Amateur Observers' Program

A few of you have already joined and submitted images, but I would
like to invite the rest of you to observe comet 9P/Tempel 1, the
target of the Deep Impact mission (deepimpact.umd.edu). Some of you
may be interested mainly in "pretty" pictures and others may wish to
contribute data... We have two observing programs...
deepimpact.umd.edu/amateur
deepimpact.umd.edu/stsp

Both require "registration" (free, it's to make sure we don't get
inappropriate images... and even after we approve a profile, we still
approve each image)...

Clear Skies!
Elizabeth Warner
warnerem@astro.umd.edu

Hello all.

I've decided to sell my Celestron 8-inch f/1.5 Schmidt Camera (with

Date:  Mon, 07 Mar 2005 00:57:03 -0000
From:  tricks46 
Subject:  9P - something different

I gathered quite a bit of information of this comet on the 5th. This
is a combination of images totaling 25 minutes. I averaged the
combination with the stack on the comet. A small gradient was fixed
and the image was saved as a tif being 800 pix wide. I then put the
image in PS and increased the size to 1200 pix wide. Adjusted the
levels, inverted and converted it to jpg and croped. Top center sits
UGC 8486 at mag 16.1 and below in the center is NGC 5162 mag 13.2.
New link at the bottom of the page at the link posted. Thanks, Mike
H.

http://hometown.aol.com/tricks46/myhomepage/photo.html?mtbrand=AOL_US

Date:  Tue, 08 Mar 2005 02:50:09 +0100
From:  "gvnn64\@libero\.it" 
Subject:  Re:[Comet-Images] 9P/Temple1 meets NGC 5162
> Was it incorrect by very much?

Hello Dennis,
the answer depends on a number of variables (like the chip and the reference stars you used, the aperture adopted to perform the photometry, and so on...). However on Mar. 6.07 I measured 9P/Tempel at R=13.6 with a coma of almost 1.1 arcmin; a magnitude of 12.5 sounds a little too much on the brighter side (discarding the outburst hypothesis).
Cheers,
Giovanni

Date:  Thu, 17 Mar 2005 21:36:51 -0000
From:  Dennis Persyk 
Subject:  62P/Tsuchinshan 1

I have been imaging 9P/Temple 1 which is brightening but the other
night it was very close to a bright star so I elected to image it's
nearest neighbor comet, 62P/Tsuchinshan 1.

62P has a relative motion of 0.5 arc seconds per minute. With my
sampling density of 2.5 arc seconds per pixel with the E160 and SXV-
H9, I was able to take 6 minute guided (on a star) subframes
without undue comet blur. 62P is about magnitude 13, a magnitude or
so dimmer than 9P, but it is also about 15 degrees higher, so less
atmospheric extinction  and skyglow, which I have lots of!

Image and supporting information at
http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

Thank you for visiting my site.

Clear skies,

Dennis Persyk
Igloo Observatory Home Page http://dpersyk.home.att.net
Hampshire, IL

Date:  Fri, 18 Mar 2005 10:05:13 +0100
From:  "gvnn64\@libero\.it" 
Subject:  Re:[Comet-Images] 62P/Tsuchinshan 1
Hello Dennis,
I imaged 62P/Tsuchinshan on March 13.99, 2005 through a 10" SC+ CCD+Cousins R filter, measuring a total magnitude close to R=13.2. There is a distinct central condensation, surrounded by a faint coma of almost 2 arcmin in diameter.
I will post a link to the image.
Cheers,
Giovanni Sostero (Remanzacco Observatory, CARA)

coma of almost 2 arcmin in diameter.
> I will post a link to the image.
> Cheers,
> Giovanni Sostero (Remanzacco Observatory, CARA)
>

Date:  Fri, 18 Mar 2005 21:39:45 -0000
From:  Terry Lovejoy 
Subject:  C/2003 T3 - March 18.77, 2005 UT

I was able to capture T3 at the very edge of some of my images this
morning. This was with just a 50mm focal length lens at f3.5 so
please excuse the poor quality as it had to be enlarged tremendously:

http://www.pbase.com/terrylovejoy/image/40947672

This looks like a great subject with a fairly prominent ion and dust
tails, but is generally being neglected. Its clearly a very dusty
object as can be seen by the yellow coma (in great contrast to 2005
A1 which is very green).

Terry

Date:  Sat, 19 Mar 2005 12:16:45 +0100
From:  "gvnn64\@libero\.it" 
Subject:  Re:[Comet-Images] 62P/Tsuchinshan 1
> Your set of images is very good. What software do you use for the
> false color image? I think it shows the tail structure even better
> than the negative.

Thanks Dennis! I normally use the "rainbow" false colors palette option of Astroart 3. As you know, the details visibility for such faint comae is strictly depending on the stretching of the image, so frequently I have to play a little with the levels (and the logarithmic strecth) to obtain a satisfactory results.
Cheers,
Giovanni

Date:  Sun, 27 Mar 2005 16:48:16 -0000
From:  tricks46 
Subject:  Old Tail Detail

Greeting to the board. The rain has not let up for weeks. I have
resorted to going back through older information in search of something
missed. 5 days before Machholz passed M45 I found almost 100mb of data
I never processed. At the time this comet was moving so quickly that
mosaics were hard to compose. The bright head of the comet also gives
problems with the fainter parts of the tail when it is in the same
frame. What I have done is not use the head and pieced to gether 25
minutes of information from 25x60 second images. Difficult because of
gradients caused by the comet along with movement. This does show what
I think is a nice tail mosaic with more detail than I have managed
before. Negative image also helps. It was a turbulant time for a
comets tail in this one. File is 550kb if you have dial up like me it
will take a minute to load. Thanks, Mike H.

http://hometown.aol.com/tricks46/myhomepage/photo.html?mtbrand=AOL_US 

Date:  Thu, 31 Mar 2005 21:04:20 +1200
From:  John_Drummond 
Subject:  Comet 2003 T4 (LINEAR) occulted by the Moon
Hi all,

have just been looking at what the 'brighter' comets are doing in April and I see that Comet 2003 T4 (LINEAR) will be covered by the Moon for about 45 minutes (according to GUIDE 8 from NZ) on Tuesday 5th April 2005 (from about 9:09 to 9:53 UT). Sadly for me in New Zealand it will be set, but there must be someone on this round planet who might see and image it. T4 may be about mag 6 and the Moon will be a thin crescent of 26 days old.

Cheers.

John Drummond
Gisborne, New Zealand
E 178* 03', S 38* 40'
email: john_drummond@xtra.co.nz
website: http://www.possumobservatory.co.nz

From:  Gerald Rhemann 
Subject:  Disconnection event on Q2 and  other one


Hi all!

We used the good weather in the last days to adjust another 8" Schmidt for CCD and to take some pictures from different comets.

comet 9P/Tempel 1, will be the target for the "Deep Impact" Mission in July and showes an inner and outer coma with a broad dust tail.

Furhter we captured 62P/Tsuchinshan and 141P/Machholz!

You can find this pictures under:

http://www.astrostudio.at/Astrofotos/astrofotos.php?kategorie=Deep-Sky click for comets and select

Also we took pictures of 2004/Q2 on April 1. 2. and 3.On April 2. the comet showed a disconnection event, which we were able to expose in color:

http://www.astrostudio.at/Astrofotos/aufnahme.php?a_id=366&page=1&kategorie=&k_id=39

A first test, under bad sky-conditions, with an ASA-Astrograph 10"/f 2.2 dated on April 3 you will find here:

http://www.astrostudio.at/Astrofotos/aufnahme.php?a_id=367&page=1&kategorie=&k_id=39

Michael J�ger und Gerald Rhemann

Date:  Mon, 04 Apr 2005 18:17:57 -0000
From:  Dennis Persyk 
Subject:  91 Days to Deep Impact  and Counting

On July 4, the NASA Deep Impact probe will crash into periodic comet
9P/Tempel 1. Hopefully the impact will cause a dramatic increase in
brightness. Meanwhile, I have been imaging 9P as it slowly wends
its way through Virgo. Images and accompanying data at
http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

Last night afforded a unique imaging opportunity: There were no
stars brighter than magnitude 15 within 3 arc minutes of 9P. That
allowed me to guide on the comet head and obtain a relatively high
statistics image of the comet structure uncluttered by star trails.
My previous comet guiding attempts were foiled when the comet would
pass over a star. My VLM was only 2.5 but long integration time
eventually wins out over light pollution.

The image comprises 8 x 10 minutes with the E160 and SXV-H9 camera.
I have added isophote and color intensity contours and some
additional supporting data to my New Images page.

My sincere thanks to Jeff Terry for loan of the H9 camera and to
Giovanni Sostero (Remanzacco Observatory, Italy) for the method of
employing the Rainbow Palette in AstroArt 3 to bring out structure.

Thank you for visiting my site.

Clear skies,

Dennis Persyk
Igloo Observatory Home Page http://dpersyk.home.att.net
Hampshire, IL

New Images http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

Date:  Thu, 07 Apr 2005 01:54:57 -0000
From:  twade35 
Subject:  Comet Machholz (C/2004 Q2) Images

These are images I took of the comet in late January through early
February 2005. I really enjoyed this comet. It's dim ion tail was
challenging to capture on film: extremely transparent skies were a
requirement. Under the most ideal conditions, I captured about a 7
degree ion tail on February 9, 2005. Here are all the links:

http://www.northwest-landscapes.com/html/comets/comet_machholz1.html
http://www.northwest-landscapes.com/html/comets/comet_machholz2.html

Date:  Thu, 14 Apr 2005 08:18:14 -0000
From:  gvnn64 
Subject:  Re: New file uploaded to Comet-Images

> I noticed the longer, straighter tail as soon as I looked at your
> image. It has inspired me to try imaging 9P tonight in spite of the
> moon and cold weather. Thanks for the "heads up" on the longer tail.

Hello Dennis,
thanks for your reply. Actually I'm not sure yet if this detail is real
or not: some fellow Italian amateurs haven't registered it in their
shots. So I was asking for some other's images. However there is a
frame of F. J. Melillo obtained on April 10, posted on the "Deep-
Impact" webpage, where this thin and straight tail is readily visible.

I was wondering if the evidence of this feature is increased when the
exposure time is low (average of multiple 30sec shots, like in my frame
and that of Melillo). I'm really puzzled about this thing: it could be
as well an artifact of some kind, or a a detail that need the
appropiate combination of contrast to be evidenced.
Cheers,
Giovanni Sostero (Remanzacco Observatory, Italy)

Date:  Fri, 15 Apr 2005 10:11:03 +0200
From:  "gvnn64\@libero\.it" 
Subject:  Re:[Comet-Images] Re: New file uploaded to Comet-Images
> Last night I tried 6 x 4 minutes guided on the head, binned 2x2 at
> f/3.3, but got only a short, fan-shaped tail looking like my image
> of a week ago. VLM 3.5 and transparency good. I was disappointed.

Hello Dennis,
thanks for your effort. In the meantime, some analysis were conduceted within the CARA team members. On some archive images secured during the last couple of weeks (in particular those obtained through B and V photometric filters) this appendage becames sometimes visible. It also seems to be aligned in the projected direction of the "theoretical" ion tail. So there is a possibility that this feature is real (the gas-tail component). It's a kind of mistery why sometimes it becames visible and others not, but if it's true, it's likely a detail very close to the noise level, and the use of filters could be helpful to discriminate it. In the next weeks we will make deeper filtered shots, in order to verify this hypothesis.
Cheers,
Giovanni Sostero (Remanzacco Observatory, CARA)

www.afamweb.com
http://cara.uai.it

Date:  Mon, 18 Apr 2005 00:46:13 +1200
From:  John_Drummond 
Subject:  Comet 2003 T4 images - 2 degree+ tail
Hi all,

Comet 2003 T4 (LINEAR) is looking nice at the moment. It's in the morning sky (rising about 2.30am local time - from NZ) and has a nice photographic tail (at least 2 degrees long). It's definitely worth getting up and having a look / taking an image.

The following are two images I took this morning - the first through my 16" f4.5 Meade Newtonian and the other through a 70-200mm Sigma lens with a 1.4x telextender (200 x 1.4 x 1.6 = 448mm @ f4).

16" -

http://www.possumobservatory.co.nz/comet_2003t4-linear-18x2min-800asa-41cm_f4,5-050416-web.jpg

or

http://tinyurl.com/7altt

Sigma lens -

http://www.possumobservatory.co.nz/comet_2003t4-linear-10x2min-800asa-448mm@f4-050416-web.jpg

or

Date:  Wed, 20 Apr 2005 18:25:31 +1200
From:  John_Drummond 
Subject:  Image of Comet 2003 T4 (LINEAR) this morning - Wed 20 April 2005
Hi all,

John Burt and I went out to a local beach here in New Zealand at about 3am this morning (Wed 20 April) to image comet 2003 T4 (LINEAR). We had a few problems - the results of which will probably be shown at the astrophotography camp (in a few weeks time) on 'what not to do' but we did get one so-so image (poorly focused though). Regardless, a tail can be seen extending for nearly 4 degrees, so we are happy...

Also attached is a twilight/sunrise image with magnitude 0.8 Mercury making its presence known.

Comet:

http://www.possumobservatory.co.nz/comet_2003t4-linear-7x3min-800asa-320mm@f2,8-050420-web.jpg

or

http://tinyurl.com/79t7c

Sunrise:

http://www.possumobservatory.co.nz/pouawa_sunrise2-b-web.jpg

or

http://tinyurl.com/9rpgj

Cheers

John D

Date:  Wed, 20 Apr 2005 22:28:16 +0930
From:  Michael Mattiazzo 
Subject:  2003 T4 + 2005 A1 images april 8th to 12th 2005
Hello all,

Finally found time to process images of both LINEAR comets, 2003 T4 and 2005
A1 and have uploaded them to my website at:

http://www.yp-connect.net/~mmatti/

Both comets appeared visually slightly better than magnitude 8.

C/2003 T4 LINEAR on April 8.79 UT 2005
20x15 second exposures, Starlight Express MX7c imager + C11 at f/3.3.
Ion-tail pointing to upper right is at least 14' in length.
Dust tail arcs towards the upper left.

C/2003 T4 LINEAR and Helix Nebula on April 8.79 UT 2005
Ion tail is close to 2 degrees long. Note the disturbance along the tail.
6x2min exposures, Canon 300D at ISO 1600 + 90mm lens.

C/2003 T4 LINEAR on April 12.81 UT 2005
5x2min exposures, Canon 300D at ISO 1600 + 205mm lens.

C/2005 A1 LINEAR on April 10.42 UT 2005
10x15 second exposures, Starlight Express MX7c imager + C11 at f/3.3.
Field of view is 15' long.

9P Tempel on April 8th 2005.
20x15 second exposures, Starlight Express MX7c imager + C11 at f/3.3.
South is up.

Cheers,
Michael Mattiazzo

Hi Michael,

Date:  Tue, 26 Apr 2005 21:29:51 +0200
From:  "gvnn64\@libero\.it" 
Subject:  Re:[Comet-Images] Apr 22 9P Images was New file uploaded to  Comet-Images
> Do you have any references to the 647 nm filter? It is not centered
> on H-a and I don't remember much about spectroscopy except that I
> didn't like to memorize selection rules for transition states.

Hello Dennis,
this filter has been selected by the CARA collaboration because it is centered in a region of the cometary spectra without noticeable emission lines. Thus it is good if you are interested on the solar continuum scattered by the dust particles within the cometary coma/tail.

You can find more informations about this technique on the CARA webpage (http://cara.uai.it).
I ordered my filter from Edmund Optics:
http://www.edmundoptics.com/onlinecatalog/DisplayProduct.cfm?productid=1903

Hope this will help.
Cheers,
Giovanni

Date:  Tue, 26 Apr 2005 22:51:48 +0200
From:  "gvnn64\@libero\.it" 
Subject:  Re:[Comet-Images] Apr 22 9P Images was New file uploaded to Comet-Images
> Looking at the specs for the filter, it seems to attenuate a lot of
> signal. May I correctly assume that is not a magic light pollution
> filter that will enhance the contrast of the tail?

Well, it surely helps cutting a lot of emission lines due to the light pollution, however as you correctly state, it also cut most of the incident light. This means that you have to take *huge* exposures for a target as faint as 9P/Tempel: in order to obtain some details, I exposed for a total of almost 1 hour with a 18" reflector. So, I'm afraid that for purely aesthetic purposes there are better solutions. A broadband R filter, is surely most suited: the dust tail of comet Tempel 1 is well visible with my Cousins R: this filter passes more signal, and cut some light pollution, as well.
Cheers,
Giovanni

The last post has me going back to some previous comets. New software
for processing and just a little more knowledge about what I am doing,
I hope,has resulted in the reprocessing of a few of the comets from the
recent past. Hope to add to this list with the time given by the
cloudy weather. Biggest problem is the filling system. Color film
negatives and not knowing how to save so the images would show decent
with the slower dialups a few years ago makes it difficult to find good
files to work with. These days I save every image taken with the
camera however big the file. Thanks Mike H.

Date:  Sun, 01 May 2005 20:25:23 -0000
From:  Dennis Persyk 
Subject:  64 Days to Deep Impact  and Counting
Many of you have read the articles in Sky and Telescope and
Astronomy magazines highlighting the upcoming Deep Impact NASA
experiment. I imaged the comet on May 1st. Images and accompanying
data at
http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

Tak E160 with SXV-H9 guided by NP101 with MX-716.

The image comprises 3 x 3 minutes and 15 x 3 with the E160 and SXV-
H9 camera, binned 2x2. I have added isophote and color intensity
contours and some additional supporting data to my New Images page.
I was surprised that the additional 36 minutes, a factor of 4
longer, did little to improve the SNR. Somehow this comet does not
seem obey statistics!

My sincere thanks to Jeff Terry for loan of the H9 camera.

Thank you for visiting my site.

Clear skies,

Dennis Persyk
Igloo Observatory Home Page http://dpersyk.home.att.net
Hampshire, IL

New Images http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

Date:  Thu, 12 May 2005 18:48:31 -0000
From:  Dennis Persyk 
Subject:  9P/Tempel 1 Rendezvous with NGC 4779
On May 10th 9P was close to NGC 4779, providing a nice photo op.
Images and accompanying data at
http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

NOAA weather radio reported an "upper level disturbance" over the
region. It sure disturbed the seeing! I had great difficulty
focusing and guiding in what seemed to be Pickering 1 skies. My
image scale of 5 arc seconds per pixel (2x2 binning at 530 mm focal
length) helped to mitigate the terrible seeing.

Thank you for visiting my site.

Clear skies,

Dennis Persyk
Igloo Observatory Home Page http://dpersyk.home.att.net
Hampshire, IL

New Images http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

Date:  Wed, 25 May 2005 16:57:19 -0000
From:  Dennis Persyk 
Subject:  C/2004 Q2 Machholz sprouted a longer tail
With a full moon I decided to image a couple of comets. I started
out with 9P Tempel 1 and then moved on to Q2 Machholz. Inspection of
the subframes of Q2 during acquisition indicated a longer tail than
I've imaged recently, bright moonlight notwithstanding. When I
processed 80 minutes of 60 second subframes I was greeted with a 27
arc minute tail. This seems longer than I've seen in recent images
of Q2. I'd appreciate any inputs as to the significance of my
seemingly longer tail observation.

I believe I've correctly oriented the image with North up/East to
left, but Guide 8 depicts the tail trailing to the Southeast.
Comments?

Image comprises 80 minutes of 60 second subframes. Takahashi E-160
f/3.3 astrograph and SXV-H9 camera on AP1200GTO mount,

Image and supporting information at
http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

Thank you for visiting my site.

Clear skies,

Dennis Persyk
Igloo Observatory Home Page http://dpersyk.home.att.net
Hampshire, IL

Date:  Sat, 28 May 2005 19:18:48 +0200
From:  "gvnn64\@libero\.it" 
Subject:  Re:[Comet-Images] DSLR images of Tempel and K2
Hello Waldemar,
your images looks really impressive, expecially if one consider that they were obtained with a telephoto lens! Comet C/2005 K2 (LINEAR) is very interesting, since it appears to be several magnitudes brighter than expected. In your shot it has a greenish hue, that is normally produced by gaseus comets; this seems to confirm some CCD photometric measurements I made yesterday night.
Cheers,
Giovanni Sostero (Remanzacco Observatory, CARA)

Date:  Mon, 06 Jun 2005 14:51:27 +0200
From:  Gerald Rhemann 
Subject:  9P/Tempel colorpicture with Astrograph
Hi!

This colorpicture of comet 9P/Tempel1 demonstrates the high performance quality of the 10"/f 2.2 Astrograph built by AstroSystemeAustria. Pinpoint stars also at the edges of the field.In combination with the quick focal ratio (varied from 2.2 to 3.8) they will be a powerful instrument for cometphotographers.

http://www.astrostudio.at/common/images/fullFormat/TempelMai3.jpg

Gerald and Michael

Date:  Tue, 07 Jun 2005 17:11:35 -0000
From:  Dennis Persyk 
Subject:  New Comet C/2005 K2 Movie
Comet movie (dial-up modem friendly) , images and supporting data at
http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

I have included a modem-friendly movie (38 kb) GIF that shows K2
streaking across 9.3 arc minutes of Ursa Major. That comet is really
racing across the FOV!

On my web page I have a color-intensity that illustrates the kind of
artifacts that one can generate with our powerful processing
software! It looks like there are jets emanating from K2. However,
the "jets" are in act nothing more than remnants from the median
combine star trails that have been smoothed out. I find that one
has to be careful in digging out faint structure in comet images
lest one create something that is really not there.

At first I was unable to find K2 in my image using SkyMap Pro with
two day old ephemeredes to get the coordinates. Then I obtained
fresh ephemeredes and the declination difference was 34 arc minutes
between new and old! The orbital parameters' accuracy must be
improving daily!

Please take a look at my images.

Clear skies,

Dennis Persyk
Igloo Observatory Home Page http://dpersyk.home.att.net
Hampshire, IL

New Images http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

Date:  Tue, 14 Jun 2005 08:42:01 +0200
From:  Maik Meyer 
Subject:  C/2005 K2 split?
Dear list,

this morning I received two images of C/2005 K2 taken by Michael Jaeger and Gerald
Rhemann on June 12. These CCD images (I'll post these tonight) showed a strange
additional condensation in direction of the short tail. However, it was not clear
if this was a condensation in the tail or maybe a chipped off part of the comets
nucleus. The feature occured in several images so it seemed not an artefact.

Michael Jaeger now points out that David Strange has also imaged this feature,
this time with larger focal length thus showing the feature more clearly.

http://www.dstrange.freeserve.co.uk/2005K2_20050613_dgs.jpg

It really seems that this comet has at least lost one larger part and this may be
responsible for the observed brightness increase. Further close monitoring
especially with larger focal lengths is strongly recommended!

Cheers, Maik
--
If they give you ruled paper, write the other way. * Juan Ramon Jimenez

maik@comethunter.de http://www.comethunter.de
German Comet Section http://www.fg-kometen.de

Date:  Tue, 14 Jun 2005 11:09:20 -0000
From:  gvnn64 
Subject:  Re: New file uploaded to Comet-Images
Hello,
this is our image obtained on Jun 13.16, 2005, where the secondary
component of C/2005 K2 (LINEAR) is already clearly visible (we have it
on sevarl nights of imaging). Later I will post other images and infos
obtained on other nights.

I sent to the CBAT the offset of this secondary condensation, as
measured on our frames: there is a noticeable drift of this splitted
part, against the main nucleus, along 24 hours. I suggest to all of you
to perform astrometry over this component, and to provide this
information to the CBAT.
Cheers,
Giovanni Sostero and Ernesto Guido (CARA)

Date:  Wed, 15 Jun 2005 19:51:58 -0000
From:  tony_p_cook 
Subject:  C/2004 Q2 + M94
Hi,

I'm new to the group so this is my first post. By way of
introduction my name is Tony Cook and I'm currently vice-president
of the UK's oldest amateur astronomical society - the Leeds
Astronomical Society.

My main interest is imaging comets and I took this activity up in
May 2004, so I'm quite new to it. I use a modest Televue 85
telescope on a Losmandy GM-8 mount and I image with a Canon EOS 10D
DSLR camera - not ideal - but its very mobile. One day I hope to do
proper CCD imaging. I've imaged quite a few comets now including:

9P Tempel 1, 78P Gehrels, C/2004 Q2, C/2004 Q1, C/2002 T7, C/2003
T4, C/2003 K4 and C/2001 Q4

Here are jsut a few of my images

http://tinyurl.com/aux9j - Machholz and M94 9th June 2005 00:00 UT
http://tinyurl.com/bqhew - Machholz 19 Dec 2004
http://tinyurl.com/ajdga - Gehrels 20 Nov 2004
http://tinyurl.com/8w27j - Tucker 21 Nov 2004
http://tinyurl.com/8y25k - Machholz 29 Jan 2005

Regards, Tony

Date:  Sat, 25 Jun 2005 10:29:03 -0000
From:  gvnn64 
Subject:  Update about C/2005 K2 (LINEAR)
Hello,
according to our images, it seems that currently the secondary
condensation of C/2005 K2 (LINEAR) -see IAUC Nr.8543- has been
vanished: our CCD shots shows in a striking manner that during these
last days, this feature became progressively fainter, broader and
diffused. On the last couple of nights it was well below the threshold
limit of our instrument (R magnitude almost 18-18.5).

In the images, there is also the hint of a possible extension of the
main object coma toward the secondary condensation (dust tail
component? Possible third fragment? Train of debris? Noise?).

It would be very interesting if anyone else would be able to follow up
the evolution of this comet; unfortunately the observing conditions
are really poor, however we will be happy to receive any additional
report about the phenomena.

Cheers,
G. Sostero and E. Guido (CARA)

Date:  Tue, 28 Jun 2005 15:43:15 -0000
From:  Dennis Persyk 
Subject:  Re: Comet Photography with a  Canon?
Hi Will,

Your question is best directed to the Digital Astro group,
http://groups.yahoo.com/group/digital_astro/

With over 9800 members they have quite a few comet photographers.

If I were buying a digital camera for comet photography, I'd get the
Canon 20Da
http://www.digitalcamerainfo.com/content/Astral-Specialized-Canon-
Coming-to-US-in-July.htm

I use a cooled CCD camera for comet photography because
1) It has about 20 times the sensitivity of a digital camera, 40
times when binned 2x2
2) Color is not important to me when imaging faint comets

The low-end SBIG camera costs less than the Canon and the
StarlightXpress cameras are in the same price range.

Clear skies,

Dennis Persyk
Igloo Observatory Home Page http://dpersyk.home.att.net
Hampshire, IL

New Images http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

Subject:  Hubble Captures Outburst from Comet Targeted By Deep Impact
not sure if you all have seen this
see below
Dr. Eric Flescher (kcstarguy@aol.com),
member, ASKC-Astronomical Society of Kansas City,

JPL / NASA Solar System Ambassador, Kansas:
Comets Observers Award Chair, Astronomical League,
http://www.astroleague.org/al/obsclubs/obsclub.html,
http://www.astroleague.org/al/obsclubs/comet/comet.htm,
http://members.aol.com/kcstarguy/satoriastronomy/cometaward.htm> or
(send messages to the network at ) CometObserversAwardAL@yahoogroups.com;

CometObserversAwardAL-subscribe@yahoogroups.com

Hubble Captures Outburst from Comet Targeted By Deep Impact
http://hubblesite.org/newscenter/newsdesk/archive/releases/2005/16/image/a
website has picture

In a dress rehearsal for the rendezvous between NASA's Deep Impact spacecraft
and comet 9P/Tempel 1, the Hubble Space Telescope captured dramatic images of
a new jet of dust streaming from the icy comet.
The images are a reminder that Tempel 1's icy nucleus, roughly half the size
of Manhattan, is dynamic and volatile. Astronomers hope the eruption of dust
seen in these observations is a preview of the fireworks that may come July 4,
when a probe from the Deep Impact spacecraft will slam into the comet,
possibly blasting off material and giving rise to a similar dust plume.
These observations demonstrate that Hubble's sharp "eye" can see exquisite
details of the comet's temperamental activities. The Earth-orbiting observatory
was 75 million miles away from the comet when these images were taken by the
Advanced Camera for Surveys' High Resolution Camera. The telescope's views
complement close-up images being taken by cameras aboard Deep Impact, which is
speeding toward the comet.
The two images, taken seven hours apart on June 14, show Tempel 1 and its
new jet. The image at left, taken at 2:17 a.m. (EDT), is a view of the comet
before the outburst. The bright dot is light reflecting from the comet's nucleus,
which appears star-like in these images because it is too small even for
Hubble to resolve. The nucleus, a potato-shaped object, is 8.7 miles (14
kilometers) wide and 2.5 miles (4 kilometers) long. Hubble's viewing the nucleus is as
difficult as someone trying to spot a potato in Salt Lake City from New York
City.
The photo at right, snapped at 9:15 a.m. (EDT), reveals the jet [the bright
fan-shaped area]. The jet extends about 1,400 miles (2,200 kilometers), which
is roughly half the distance across the U.S. It is pointing in the direction
of the Sun. Comets frequently show outbursts in activity, but astronomers still
don't know exactly why they occur. Tempel 1 has been moving closer to the
Sun, and perhaps the increasing heat opened up a crack in the comet's dark,
crusty surface. Dust and gas trapped beneath the surface could then spew out of the
crack, forming a jet. Or, perhaps a portion of the crust itself was lifted
off the nucleus by the pressure of heated gases beneath the surface. This porous
crust might then crumble into small dust particles shortly after leaving the
nucleus, producing a fan-shaped coma on the sunward side. Whatever the cause,
the new feature may not last for long.
Astronomers hope that the July 4 collision will unleash more primordial
material trapped inside the comet, which formed billions of years ago. Comets are
thought to be "dirty snowballs," porous agglomerates of ice and rock that
dwell in the frigid outer boundaries of our solar system. Periodically, they make
their journey into the inner solar system as they loop around the Sun.
The contrast in these images has been enhanced to highlight the brightness
of the new jet.
Contacts:
Ray Villard Space Telescope Science Institute, Baltimore, MD (Phone:
410-338-4514; E-mail: villard@stsci.edu)
Paul Feldman Johns Hopkins University, Baltimore, MD (Phone: 410-516-7339;
E-mail: pdf@pha.jhu.edu)
Hal Weaver Johns Hopkins University/Applied Physics Lab, Laurel, MD (Phone:
443-778-8078; E-mail: hal.weaver@jhuapl.edu)
Lars Lindberg Christensen Hubble European Space Agency Information Centre,
Garching, Germany (Phone: +49-(0)89-3200-6306; Cell: +49-(0)173-3872-621;
E-mail: lars@eso.org)
Credit: NASA, ESA, P. Feldman (Johns Hopkins University), and H. Weaver
(Johns Hopkins University/Applied Physics Lab)
Image Type: Astronomical
STScI-PRC2005-16

Images labeled as "Print" are designed to print on standard, letter-size
paper. We recommend that the casual user use the PDF version (if available),
which sizes the image to fit on a single sheet of paper. In order to use the PDF
image, you will need to install Acrobat reader, which is available free from
Adobe. The JPEG version of this image (if available) is best suited for
experienced users who are familiar with image and printer resolutions.

To set an image as your background, click on the link of the desired image
and resolution. Once it has loaded, right-click on the image (control-click on
a Macintosh) and select "Set as Background" or "Set as Wallpaper" from the
menu.

"Highest Quality" images are the best images available. There are no higher
quality images. Astronomical images may vary in size and resolution for
several reasons: different cameras have detectors of different pixel size, images
may be cropped from larger fields, and the images may consist of mosaics of
several images.

> 5194 (= M51) (Koichi Itagaki confirmation image) (LOSS image) Mag
13.5, Type unknown
> (References: SN 1994I)

That sure is a bright supernova, I was looking at the images on this
website they are pretty good:

http://personales.jet.es/drodrig/super.htm

* How to Watch July 4 Comet Impact
http://www.space.com/spacewatch/050603_deep_impact.html

In early July, NASA's Deep Impact spacecraft will deploy a tiny impactor to
smash into the nucleus of a small comet. The idea is to excavate a sizable
crater and provide valuable insight into the true nature of comets.

COMET TEMPEL 1 UPDATE

It's less than a month until NASA's Deep Impact mission sends a projectile
to blast the icy surface of Comet Tempel 1. The comet is currently quite
faint (10th magnitude) in the evening sky, appearing large but with a low
surface brightness according to observers in the last few days. Details
and finder charts:

http://SkyandTelescope.com/observing/highlights/article_1522_1.asp

* Cosmic Crash Won't Destroy Comet or Earth
http://www.space.com/scienceastronomy/050628_deepimpact_effect.html

Late Sunday and early Monday, skywatchers might be treated to celestial
fireworks unlike anything witnessed before as NASA's Deep Impact spacecraft tangles
with Comet Tempel 1 on July 4.
Advance Fireworks Shoot from Comet NASA Will Hit
http://www.space.com/scienceastronomy/050627_hubble_comet.html

A comet that will be whacked by a NASA probe next week almost looks like its
having some pre-impact jitters, flaring up in a new picture taken by the
Hubble Space Telescope.

Date:  Mon, 04 Jul 2005 00:16:08 +1200
From:  John_Drummond 
Subject:  OBS-V: Tempel 1 - 9p
Hi all,

here's two visual observations I made of Comet 9P last week - and a photo through the 16".

Comet Obs:

P/Tempel 1 (9P) 2005 June 28.31 UT: m1 = 9.9, Dia.= 3'.5, DC = 4, 8cm f5 Refract (18x)...John Drummond, (Gisborne, New Zealand) [Based on John Bortle's comments that the smallest possible aperture should be used for comet magnitude estimates I used a 8cm f5 refractor. Can see the comet - but not easily. Through the 16" it is easily seen - the coma is diffuse with slight central condensation. 58* alt in the NNW. Stellar LM =11.3 (8cm)].

P/Tempel 1 (9P) 2005 June 29.29 UT: m1 = 9.8, Dia.= 3'.5, DC = 3, 8cm f5 Refract (18x)...John Drummond, (Gisborne, New Zealand) [Can just see 9P. Slight high cloud. Through the 16" it is easily seen - the coma seems slightly more diffuse than last night - less central condensation. 58* alt in the N. Stellar LM =11.1 (8cm)].

JPL (NASA) -

P/Tempel 1 (9P) 2005 June 28.31 UT: m1=9.9, Dia.=3'.5, DC=4/9, 8cm f5 Refract (18x)...John Drummond, (Gisborne, New Zealand) [Based on John Bortle's comments that the smallest possible aperture should be used for comet magnitude estimates I used a 8cm f5 refractor. Can see the comet - but not easily. Through the 16" it is easily seen - the coma is diffuse with slight central condensation. 58* alt in the NNW. Stellar LM =11.3 (8cm)].

P/Tempel 1 (9P) 2005 June 29.29 UT: m1=9.8, Dia.=3'.5, DC=3/9, 8cm f5 Refract (18x)...John Drummond, (Gisborne, New Zealand) [Can just see 9P. Slight high cloud. Through the 16" it is easily seen - the coma seems slightly more diffuse than last night - less central condensation. 58* alt in the N. Stellar LM =11.1 (8cm)].

An image through my 16" f4.5 can be seen at:

http://www.possumobservatory.co.nz/c-009p-tempel1-050627-26x1&3min@800asa-41cm-f4,5-web.jpg

or

http://tinyurl.com/bhgfh

Date:  Tue, 05 Jul 2005 15:48:29 +0200
From:  "gvnn64\@libero\.it" 
Subject:  Split of C/2005A1 (LINEAR)
Hello all,
it seems that we are living a season of splitting comets: we can confirm the presence of a secondary component within the coma of C/2005 A1 (LINEAR), as reported on M.P.E.C. 2005-N21: on a series of CCD images we obtained remotely on July 5.41, 2005, using a 0.25m f/3.4 reflector (near Mayhill, NM), it's possible to see a secondary condensation (R = 16.5) placed in PA 210 deg, almost 21 arcsec away from the main nuclues. This component show a small tail almost 20 arcsec long toward PA 250 deg, and follows the proper motion of the primary component. The primary component show a total magnitude of almost R= 12.5 (coma diameter almost 1.4 arcmin) and a nice tail 2' long toward South-West.

An image link will be posted soon...

Kind regards,
Giovanni Sostero and Ernesto Guido

Subject:  brightness curve for comet Tempel 1
Hello: I was fortunate to be able to acquire a series of 2-minute
images of comet Tempel 1 during the impact Sunday evening (California
time). While the comet itself was not much to look at, and the
visual changes subtle, CCD imaging revealed a dramatic increase in
brightness. The following link contains the story and accompanying
images. As always, comments welcome

-Mike

http://psych102-143.ucsd.edu/~Mike/cometimpact.html

Date:  Wed, 06 Jul 2005 13:24:35 -0400
From:  KCStarguy@aol.com
Subject:  image of comet Tempel brightening after impact
fyi

at http://spaceweather.com/
take a look and see the picture of comet Tempel 1 just before the impactor
struck it - craters and more
see also at
http://antwrp.gsfc.nasa.gov/apod/astropix.html
then below is the link for the gallery

at this site is the latest deep impact pictures
http://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/deepimpact/main/index.html
then this more below the link

Here on Earth, the "brilliant flash" was hidden from direct view by Tempel
1's huge atmosphere. Indeed, the appearance of the comet hardly changed at
first. But, eventually, the comet did brighten as the impact plume billowed out of
the crater. The plume did not glow on its own, but rather reflected sunlight
to Earth, more and more as the plume expanded.

(see this pic on spaceweather)

This image from a 14-inch telescope in Arizona is an example of the view
from Earth. It consists of 25 exposures spanning 2 hours and 40 minutes with the
impact in the middle. There was no sudden flash, but a gradual, sustained
increase in the comet's overall brightness. The total change amounted to about 2
magnitudes. Image credit: Rocky Alvey, Terry Mann, A.G. Kasselberg and Richard
Berry of Vanderbilt University and the Astronomical League.
Contrary to expectations, Comet Tempel 1 did not become a naked-eye object
as a result of the collision. A telescope is still required to view the comet:
observing tips.

Date:  Sat, 09 Jul 2005 23:33:57 -0000
From:  Dennis Persyk 
Subject:  161P Hartley-IRAS


Periodic comet 161 P is now among the brightest comets in the
northern skies at magnitude 10.1. It is well positioned in
Cassiopeia. It has a relative motion of 3.4 arc seconds per minute,
making it a good movie candidate.

My mage comprises 30 minutes of 120 second subframes. Takahashi E-
160 f/3.3 astrograph and SXV-H9 camera on AP1200GTO mount.

A modem-friendly movie and supporting data are included on my web
page below  please take a look.

Image and supporting information at
http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

Thank you for visiting my site.

Clear skies,

Dennis Persyk
Igloo Observatory Home Page http://dpersyk.home.att.net
Hampshire, IL

Date:  Sun, 10 Jul 2005 23:48:45 -0400

Subject:  latest results on deep impact findings

Message: 1
   Date: Fri, 8 Jul 2005 15:07:19 -0700 (PDT)
   From: Ron Baalke
Subject: Scientists Measure How Deep 'Deep Impact' Was, With X-Rays

http://live.psu.edu/story/12671

Scientists measure how deep 'Deep Impact' was, with X-rays
Pennsylvania State University
July 8, 2005

University Park, Pa. -- Here come the X-rays, on cue. Scientists
studying the Deep Impact collision using NASA's Swift satellite report
that comet Tempel 1 is getting brighter and brighter in X-ray light with
each passing day.

The X-rays provide a direct measurement of how much material was kicked
up in the impact. This is because the X-rays are created by the newly
liberated material lifted into the comet's thin atmosphere and
illuminated by the high-energy solar wind from the sun. The more
material liberated, the more X-rays are produced.

Swift data of the water evaporation on comet Tempel 1 also may provide
new insights into how solar wind can strip water from planets such as Mars.

"Prior to its rendezvous with the Deep Impact probe, the comet was a
rather dim X-ray source," said Paul O'Brien of the Swift team at the
University of Leicester. "How things change when you ram a comet with a
copper probe traveling over 20,000 miles per hour. Most of the X-ray
light we detect now is generated by debris created by the collision. We
can get a solid measurement of the amount of material released."

"It takes several days after an impact for surface and sub-surface
material to reach the comet's upper atmosphere, or coma," said Dick
Willingale, also of the University of Leicester. "We expect the X-ray
production to peak this weekend. Then we will be able to assess how much
comet material was released from the impact."

Based on preliminary X-ray analysis, O'Brien estimates that several tens
of thousands of tons of material were released, enough to bury Penn
State's football field under 30 feet of comet dust. Observations and
analysis are ongoing at the Swift Mission Operations Center at Penn
State as well as in Italy and the United Kingdom.

Swift is providing the only simultaneous multi-wavelength observation of
this rare event, with a suite of instruments capable of detecting
visible light, ultraviolet light, X-rays and gamma rays. Different
wavelengths reveal different secrets about the comet.

The Swift team hopes to compare the satellite's ultraviolet data,
collected hours after the collision, with the X-ray data. The
ultraviolet light was created by material entering into the lower region
of the comet's atmosphere; the X-rays come from the upper regions. Swift
is a nearly ideal observatory for making these comet studies, as it
combines both a rapidly responsive scheduling system with both X-ray and
optical/UV instruments in the same satellite.

"For the first time, we can see how material liberated from a comet's
surface migrates to the upper reaches of its atmosphere," said John
Nousek, director of Mission Operations at Penn State. "This will provide
fascinating information about a comet's atmosphere and how it interacts
with the solar wind. This is all virgin territory."

Nousek said Deep Impact's collision with comet Tempel 1 is like a
controlled laboratory experiment of the type of slow evaporation process
from solar wind that took place on Mars. The Earth has a magnetic field
that shields us from solar wind, a particle wind composed mostly of
protons and electrons moving at nearly light speed. Mars lost its
magnetic field billions of years ago, and the solar wind stripped the
planet of water.

Comets, like Mars and Venus, have no magnetic fields. Comets become
visible largely because ice is evaporated from their surface with each
close passage around the Sun. Water is dissociated into its component
atoms by the bright sunlight and swept away by the fast-moving and
energetic solar wind. Scientists hope to learn about this evaporation
process on Tempel 1 now occurring quickly -- over the course of a few
weeks instead of a billion years -- as the result of a planned, human
intervention.

Swift's "day job" is detecting distant, natural explosions called
gamma-ray bursts and creating a map of X-ray sources in the universe.
Swift's extraordinary speed and agility enable scientists to follow
Tempel 1 day by day to see the full effect from the Deep Impact collision.

For the latest news on Swift analysis of comet Tempel 1, refer to:
-- http://www.science.psu.edu/alert/Swift-Deep-Impact.htm
-- http://swift.gsfc.nasa.gov and
-- http://swift.sonoma.edu/

The Deep Impact mission is managed by NASA's Jet Propulsion Laboratory,
Pasadena, Calif. Swift is a medium-class NASA explorer mission in
partnership with the Italian Space Agency and the Particle Physics and
Astronomy Research Council in the United Kingdom, and is managed by NASA
Goddard. Penn State controls science and flight operations from the
Mission Operations Center in University Park. The spacecraft was built
in collaboration with national laboratories, universities and
international partners, including Penn State University; Los Alamos
National Laboratory, N.M.; Sonoma State University, Rohnert Park,
Calif.; Mullard Space Science Laboratory in Dorking, Surrey, England;
the University of Leicester, England; Brera Observatory in Milan, Italy;
and ASI Science Data Center in Frascati, Italy.

Contact
  Barbara Kennedy
  science@psu.edu
  www.science.psu.edu
  814-863-4682

Message: 2
   Date: Fri, 8 Jul 2005 16:27:35 -0700 (PDT)
   From: Ron Baalke
Subject: NASA's Deep Impact Tells a Tale of the Comet

Dolores Beasley
Headquarters, Washington                        July 8, 2005
(Phone: 202/358-1753)

DC Agle
Jet Propulsion Laboratory, Pasadena, Calif.
(Phone: 818/393-9011)

Lee Tune
University of Maryland, College Park, Md.
(Phone:  301/405-4679)

RELEASE: 05-177

NASA'S DEEP IMPACT TELLS A TALE OF THE COMET

Data from Deep Impact's instruments indicate an immense
cloud of fine powdery material was released when the probe
slammed into the nucleus of comet Tempel 1 at 6.3 miles per
second.

The cloud indicated the comet is covered in the powdery stuff.
The Deep Impact science team continues to wade through
gigabytes of data collected during the July 4 encounter with
the 3-mile-wide by 7-mile-long comet.

"The major surprise was the opacity of the plume the impactor
created and the light it gave off," said Deep Impact Principal
Investigator Dr. Michael A'Hearn of the University of Maryland,
College Park. "That suggests the dust excavated from the
comet's surface was extremely fine, more like talcum powder
than beach sand. And the surface is definitely not what most
people think of when they think of comets -- an ice cube."

How can a comet hurtling through our solar system be made of a
substance with less strength than snow or even talcum powder?
"You have to think of it in the context of its environment,"
said Pete Schultz, Deep Impact scientist from Brown University,
Providence, R.I. "This city-sized object is floating around in
a vacuum. The only time it gets bothered is when the sun cooks
it a little or someone slams an 820-pound wakeup call at it at
23,000 miles per hour."

The data review process is not overlooking a single frame of
approximately 4,500 images from the spacecraft's three imaging
cameras taken during the encounter. "We are looking at
everything from the last moments of the impactor to the final
look-back images taken hours later, and everything in between,"
added A'Hearn. "Watching the last moments of the impactor's
life is remarkable. We can pick up such fine surface detail
that objects that are only four meters in diameter can be made
out. That is nearly a factor of 10 better than any previous
comet mission."

The final moments of the impactor's life are important,
because they set the stage for all subsequent scientific
findings. Knowing the location and angle the impactor
slammed into the comet's surface is the best place to start.
Engineers have established the impactor took two not unexpected
coma particle hits prior to impact. The impacts slewed the
spacecraft's camera for a few moments before the attitude
control system could get it back on track. The penetrator hit
at an approximately 25 degree oblique angle relative to the
comet's surface. That's when the fireworks began.

The fireball of vaporized impactor and comet material shot
skyward. It expanded rapidly above the impact site at
approximately 3.1 miles per second, and the crater was just
beginning to form. Scientists are still analyzing the data
to determine the exact size of the crater. Scientists say
the crater was at the large end of original expectations,
which was from 50 to 250 meters.

Expectations for Deep Impact's flyby spacecraft were exceeded
during its close brush with the comet. The craft is more
than 2.2 million miles from Tempel 1 and opening the distance
at approximately 23,000 mph. The flyby spacecraft is
undergoing a thorough checkout, and all systems appear to
be in excellent operating condition.

The Deep Impact mission was implemented to provide a glimpse
beneath the surface of a comet, where material from the solar
system's formation remains relatively unchanged. Mission
scientists hoped the project would answer basic questions
about the formation of the solar system by providing an
in-depth picture of the nature and composition of comets.

The University of Maryland is responsible for overall Deep
Impact mission science, and project management is handled by
JPL. The spacecraft was built for NASA by Ball Aerospace &
Technologies Corporation, Boulder, Colo. For information
about Deep Impact on the Internet, visit:

http://www.nasa.gov/deepimpact

For information about NASA and agency programs on the Web,
visit:

http://www.nasa.gov/home/index.html

-end-

Message: 3
   Date: Fri, 8 Jul 2005 16:38:38 -0700 (PDT)
   From: Ron Baalke
Subject: Deep Impact Was a Dust-up, Not a Gusher

http://www.cfa.harvard.edu/press/pr0523.html

Harvard-Smithsonian Center for Astrophysics

Press Release No.: 05-23
For Immediatie Release: July 8, 2005

Deep Impact Was a Dust-up, Not a Gusher

Cambridge, MA - Smithsonian astronomers watched as the "Impactor" probe
from NASA's Deep Impact spacecraft hit Comet Tempel 1 earlier this week.
They monitored the impact using the ground-based Submillimeter Array
(SMA) in Hawaii and NASA's orbiting Submillimeter Wave Astronomy
Satellite (SWAS). Results are still coming in, but so far the scientists
report seeing only weak emission from water vapor and a host of other
gases that were expected to erupt from the impact site. The most
conspicuous feature of the blast was brightening due to sunlight
scattered by the ejected dust.

"It's pretty clear that this event did not produce a gusher," said SWAS
principal investigator Gary Melnick of the Harvard-Smithsonian Center
for Astrophysics (CfA). "The more optimistic predictions for water
output from the impact haven't materialized, at least not yet."

Astronomer Charlie Qi (CfA) expressed surprise at these results. He
explained that short-period comets like Tempel 1 have been baked
repeatedly by the sun during their passages through the inner solar
system. The effects of that heat are estimated to extend more than three
feet beneath the surface of the nucleus. But the Deep Impact indicates
that these effects could be much deeper.

"Theories about the volatile layers below the surface of short-period
comets are going to have to be revised," Qi said.

As seen from Earth, a comet typically displays a fuzzy round head and a
glowing tail. Both the head and tail consist of gases and dust ejected
from the comet's nucleus - a frozen chunk of rock and ice about half the
size of Manhattan Island.

Five decades ago, Harvard astronomer Fred Whipple developed a model of
comet nuclei as "dirty snowballs." He hypothesized that comets consist
of mostly ice with some dirt and rock mixed in. Modern astronomers often
refer to comets as "icy dirtballs" instead, reflecting the prevailing
view that comets contain more dust and less ice than previously believed.

Deep Impact was intended to test these theories by excavating material
from the comet's interior, giving scientists clues to its composition
and structure. The mission succeeded admirably, pulverizing a section of
the comet larger than a house and releasing tons of material into space.

SWAS operators were puzzled by the lack of increased water vapor from
Tempel 1. Post-impact measurements showed the comet was releasing only
about 550 pounds of water per second-an emission rate very similar to
pre-impact values, and less than seen by SWAS during natural outbursts
in the weeks before the impact.

SMA measurements corroborate the SWAS findings. Although the SMA wasn't
tuned to frequencies of water emission, which are difficult to observe
from the ground due to atmospheric water vapor, it watched for other
chemicals such as hydrogen cyanide. SMA astronomers saw little increase
in production of gases following the impact. Gas production rates
remained so low that they could set only an upper limit on the total.

"All we needed was a factor of three boost from the impact to get a
definite detection," said Qi. "We didn't see that."

Qi added that the comet might become more active over the following days
and weeks. "We're still hoping for a big outgassing from the new active
area created by Deep Impact. If we see any signs of that, we'll make
more observations."

The researchers will continue their careful and detailed analysis in
order to interpret the SMA and SWAS measurements and what they indicate
about the comet's composition.

"The big picture will emerge once astronomers meld data from different
observatories at different wavelengths," said Melnick.

Headquartered in Cambridge, Mass., the Harvard-Smithsonian Center for
Astrophysics (CfA) is a joint collaboration between the Smithsonian
Astrophysical Observatory and the Harvard College Observatory. CfA
scientists, organized into six research divisions, study the origin,
evolution and ultimate fate of the universe.

For more information, contact:

David Aguilar, Director of Public Affairs
Harvard-Smithsonian Center for Astrophysics
Phone: 617-495-7462 Fax: 617-495-7468
daguilar@cfa.harvard.edu

Christine Pulliam
Public Affairs Specialist
Harvard-Smithsonian Center for Astrophysics
Phone: 617-495-7463, Fax: 617-495-7016
cpulliam@cfa.harvard.edu

Date:  Mon, 25 Jul 2005 17:11:39 -0400
From:  KCStarguy@aol.com
Subject:  Deep Impact: Before and After

Deep Impact: Before and After

http://www.universetoday.com/am/publish/deep_impact_during_and_after_caltech.html?2572005
Jul 25, 2005 - The powerful Palomar observatory has released its before and
after images of Comet Tempel 1 after it was struck by Deep Impact. In the
earliest images taken after impact, the photos show a plume of material extending
320 km (200 miles) from the comet's centre. This dust plume lasted for several
nights, and allowed astronomers to measure its rotation. Two days later
another, smaller plume was visible, which could have vented from an outburst of gas
and dust coming from the impact site.

Full Story

Dr. Eric Flescher (kcstarguy@aol.com),
member, ASKC-Astronomical Society of Kansas City,
Olathe, KS. USA,
JPL / NASA Solar System Ambassador, Kansas:
Comets Observers Award Chair, Astronomical League,
http://www.astroleague.org/al/obsclubs/obsclub.html,
http://www.astroleague.org/al/obsclubs/comet/comet.htm,

Date:  Tue, 02 Aug 2005 21:29:51 -0000
From:  Dennis Persyk 
Subject:  Comet Movie Starring 161P
The fast relative motion of 2.9 arc seconds per minute makes for an
interesting imaging challenge and a great movie opportunity. Images
comprise 52 x 2 minutes unguided (too many stars in the comets' path
to guide!) with the E-160 and SXV-H9 on an AP1200GTO.

Movie and supporting information at
http://home.att.net/~dpersyk/new.htm

Thank you for visiting my site.

Clear skies,

Dennis Persyk
Igloo Observatory Home Page http://dpersyk.home.att.net
Hampshire, IL

Date:  Mon, 08 Aug 2005 22:18:22 -0000
From:  Rick Wiggins 
Subject:  Best piggyback position for Perseids
Hi,
I would like to attempt to capture a color image of a meteor during the
upcoming peak of Perseids. I will be mounting a Canon 20D with either a
20mm or 50mm lens piggybacked on a AP mount. I will probably be taking
CCD images at the same time.

Is it best to point the camera at the radiant or somewhere away from
the radiant and if so, how far in degrees?

Any other tips would be greatly appreciated.
Thanks, Rick 

Date:  Mon, 08 Aug 2005 17:39:37 -0500
From:  "P. Clay Sherrod" 
Subject:  Re: [Comet-Images] Best piggyback position for Perseids
Hi Rick..
By all means do not point at the radiant; the best show will always be where you are not
looking, but find one part of the sky and stay with it pretty much for the duration if you
can. I always recommend directly overhead from about 11 p.m. to 2 a.m. and then shifting
the camera to a half-way point between overhead and the western or southwestern horizon
for the rest of the morning.....best of success!

Dr. Clay
--------------------
Arkansas Sky Observatories
Harvard MPC H41 (Petit Jean Mountain)
Harvard MPC H45 (Petit Jean Mountain South)
Harvard MPC H43 (Conway)
Harvard MPC H44 (Cascade Mountain)
http://www.arksky.org/
