Date:  Tue, 09 Aug 2005 18:25:07 +0000
From:  Bernd Brinkmann 
Subject:  Re: [Comet-Images] Best piggyback position for Perseids
Hello Rick,

> I would like to attempt to capture a color image of a meteor during the
> upcoming peak of Perseids. I will be mounting a Canon 20D with either a
> 20mm or 50mm lens piggybacked on a AP mount. I will probably be taking
> CCD images at the same time.

if you have the choice, take the shortest focal length you have. I
photographed the Perseids last year with an 10D and 300D with a 8mm
and 15mm focal length. The result: 15 mm 14 perseids plus 9 sporadics
and 8 mm 22 perseids and 2 sparadics during the whole night with 112
sec exposures each 2 min (guided on the same mount).
>
> Is it best to point the camera at the radiant or somewhere away from the
> radiant and if so, how far in degrees?

With 8 mm it doesn't matter, because it's near all sky. But we pointed
to the zenit with a slight direction to the eastern horizon.

Clear skies
Bernd

Bernd Brinkmann
Sternwarte Herne, MPC code A18

http://www.sternwarte-herne.de
info@sternwarte-herne.de 

Date:  Thu, 11 Aug 2005 17:03:32 -0000
From:  Rick Wiggins 
Subject:  Re: Best piggyback position for Perseids
Thanks,
I will take your advice.
Rick

Date:  Sun, 21 Aug 2005 14:07:22 -0700
From:  Charles Bell 
Subject:  C/2005 A1 fragment separation
Image of C/2005 A1 showing both fragments.

Fainter b fragment was just south of the brighter a
fragment.

Measured/calculated separation of 26.9 arc seconds.

http://www.quantumhyperspace.com/imagefiles/C2005A1-08192005-1028UT-13x120s.JPG

Imaged August 19,2005 0936 to 1028 UT
13 x 120 second exposures

The moon was very low behind trees. Comet near
overhead in clear skies.

Heaven's Gate Observatory H47
32 15' 59"N, 90 51' 22"W
http://www.quantumhyperspace.com/ 

Date:  Sun, 21 Aug 2005 16:36:39 -0500
From:  "P. Clay Sherrod" 
Subject:  Re: [Comet-Images] C/2005 A1 fragment separation
It is interesting that this apparent separation between the two components is essentially
the same as it was about three weeks ago when we last measured the spacing on the two
components. There seems to be no differential velocity in the two bodies over quite a
long period of time.

Dr. Clay

Date:  Sat, 27 Aug 2005 20:16:21 -0000
From:  Charles Bell 
Subject:  Re: C/2005 A1 fragment separation
An ephemeris generated for the month of August 2005 shows that
C/2005 A1 (LINEAR) passed the closest distance to earth (delta)
during the week prior to 8/19/2005.

Calculated separation distance ~ 30511 km

delta = 1.5831726175 AU
1 AU= 149597870.691 km
r dtheta = 30511 km

>From http://ssd.jpl.nasa.gov/cgi-bin/eph

*********************************************************************
*************

diam Ob-lon Ob-lat r rdot delta deldot
1-way_LT S-T-O
*********************************************************************
*************
2005-Aug-28 10:19 Am 00 23 28.62 +28 06 25.1 13.33 n.a.
n.a. n.a. n.a. 2.3894792331 21.47533 1.5831726175 6.44878
13.166845 18.0008

Date:  Sun, 09 Oct 2005 04:17:18 -0000
From:  tricks46 
Subject:  An Older Comet Image
Greetings. Over the past few months I have revisited some older comet
images. I was never really satisified with the outcome of some images,
so I have been trying some new process from around the web most which
are applied to deep space images. The fast movement of comets usually
does not allow for straight forward processing so I had to pick and
choose to see what might work and use some of my own ideas. Most of
this was on the head portion trying to keep the overexposed areas
down. Anyway I though I would process and publish one of these images
here.
This is Q2 from January 05. File is about 650kb.
Thanks, Mike H.

http://www.fototime.com/85C0EF6B904ED4F/orig.jpg 

Date:  Wed, 12 Oct 2005 23:27:25 -0000
From:  mackjulian 
Subject:  Re: C/2005 A1 (LINEAR)
After seeing your nice image I thought I would give it a try, only
clear night in weeks here in New Mexico.

http://myweb.cableone.net/mackjulian/c2005A1-version2.gif

You can see the split nucleus, didn't know about it till I stacked
them and then went back to your image/site and read about it having a
split nucleus.

Imaged with ST-10XME bin2 0.95asp, stack of 31x60sec images, 0.30m SCT
f/10 on Paramount ME.

http://myweb.cableone.net/mackjulian/c2005A1.gif

This last image is the same thing but I tried to layer the stacked
image on the first frame so the stars are not trailed and stacked
comet images comes thru. Still learning to use photoshop...

Mack

Date:  Wed, 26 Oct 2005 01:01:44 +1300
From:  John Drummond 
Subject:  Obs/image of C/2005 T4 (SWAN)
Hi all,

well I tried to visually see newly discovered C/2005 T4 (SWAN) a few hours ago in the western sky. I didn't definitely see it, but thought I saw a VERY faint smudge at a spot different to the predicted position (read below). I also imaged it...

C/2005 T4 (SWAN)

2005 October 25.34 UT: m1 = ~12.0 - 13.0, Dia.= -, DC = -, 41cm f4.5 Reflect (83x). John Drummond, (Gisborne, New Zealand) [New Comet SWAN (C/2005 T4) was not definately seen visually. It's very low in the western sky <8* above the horizon. Can see stars to mag 13.5. Interestingly I THOUGHT at times I could detect a FAINT smudge about 4' to the NW of mag 10.5 star TYC 563143 but dismissed it due to it being in a different location than the predicted one (on GUIDE using the IAU ephemerides) - it was that faint and vague that I thought it was just my eyes/mind playing tricks on me. However exposures show the smudges location to be the comet! Based on these images as at 25.36 October (UT) I get a position of RA 16h 33m 05s, Dec 09h 55m 57s. I guess the m1 visual mag was ~12.0 - 13.0. Imaged T4 when it was only 3* altitude in the west. Stellar VLM = 13.5 (scope)].

An image (comet was only about 3 degrees above the horizon) can be seen at:



or

http://tinyurl.com/88fbn

Cheers.

John Drummond
Gisborne, New Zealand
E 178* 03', S 38* 40'
email: john_drummond@xtra.co.nz
website: http://www.possumobservatory.co.nz

Nice image Mike, I managed to see it visually in my 12.5" f/5.6
reflector from the light polluted skies of Ann Arbor, MI on November
20th at about 18:45 EST. It appear as a small almost stellar spot
just about 5' due west of a 6.8 magnitude star. I could just make out
a little diffused coma extending about 2.5' north east.
You are right about it being the best show in town, but you'll have
to admit its been a great year for comets.

Date:  Sat, 17 Dec 2005 17:20:40 -0000
From:  Charles Bell 
Subject:  Re: C/2005 E2 on December 12, 2005 0058UT and December 14, 2005 0036UT
Thanks for the nice comments Mike. The ST-2000XCM is my first real
CCD camera. It was a giant step up from a Meade imager. The addition
of the AO-7 finally allowed much longer self guided exposures. The
periodic error in the Meade mount has been the biggest challenge.

I have been imaging at f/10. Over the last few months I was able to
image fainter comets like 29P, C/2003 K4, C/2005A1, and P/2005 R2.

To go deeper I will have to try a focal reducer. There were
troublesome internal reflections the last time I tried the Meade
F/6.3 FR. Perhaps I will try the camera with the F/3.3 focal reducer
but without the AO-7.

I have not seen anything to suggest a bent tail on C/2005 E2. All I
see is a basic symmetric radial spread of light emanating from the
comet. The spread seems to be approximately perpendicular to the
direction of apparent movement.
